class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n<div class="mw-indicator" id="mw-indicator-featured-star"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Featured_articles" title="This is a featured article. Click here for more information." data-file-height="438" data-file-width="462" decoding="async" height="19" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/20px-Cscr-featured.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/30px-Cscr-featured.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Cscr-featured.svg/40px-Cscr-featured.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></div>\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Supernova</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Supernova_(disambiguation)" title="Supernova (disambiguation)">Supernova (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Star exploding at the end of its stellar lifespan</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SN1994D.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2608" data-file-width="2608" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/SN1994D.jpg/260px-SN1994D.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/SN1994D.jpg/390px-SN1994D.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/SN1994D.jpg/520px-SN1994D.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SN1994D.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/SN_1994D" title="SN 1994D">SN 1994D</a> (bright spot on the lower left), a <a href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernova" title="Type Ia supernova">Type Ia supernova</a> within its host galaxy, <a href="/wiki/NGC_4526" title="NGC 4526">NGC 4526</a></div></div></div>\n<p>A <b>supernova</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#716;/: secondary stress follows">&#716;</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span><span title="/u&#720;/: \'oo\' in \'goose\'">u&#720;</span><span title="\'p\' in \'pie\'">p</span><span title="/&#601;r/: \'er\' in \'letter\'">&#601;r</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/o&#650;/: \'o\' in \'code\'">o&#650;</span><span title="\'v\' in \'vie\'">v</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span></span>/</a></span></span> plural: <b>supernovae</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#716;/: secondary stress follows">&#716;</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span><span title="/u&#720;/: \'oo\' in \'goose\'">u&#720;</span><span title="\'p\' in \'pie\'">p</span><span title="/&#601;r/: \'er\' in \'letter\'">&#601;r</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span><span title="/o&#650;/: \'o\' in \'code\'">o&#650;</span><span title="\'v\' in \'vie\'">v</span><span title="/i&#720;/: \'ee\' in \'fleece\'">i&#720;</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <b>supernovas</b>, abbreviations: <b>SN</b> and <b>SNe</b>) is an event that occurs upon the death of certain types of stars.\n</p><p>Supernovae are more energetic than <a href="/wiki/Nova" title="Nova">novae</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>, <i>nova</i> means "new", referring astronomically to what appears to be a temporary new bright star. Adding the prefix "super-" distinguishes supernovae from ordinary novae, which are far less luminous. The word <i>supernova</i> was coined by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Baade" title="Walter Baade">Walter Baade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Fritz Zwicky</a> in 1931.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baas33_1330_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-baas33_1330-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Only three <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, naked-eye supernova events have been observed during the last thousand years, though many have been seen in other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galaxies" title="Galaxies">galaxies</a>. The most recent directly observed supernova in the Milky Way was <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_Supernova" title="Kepler\'s Supernova">Kepler\'s Supernova</a> in 1604, but two more recent <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">supernova remnants</a> have also been found. Statistical observations of supernovae in other galaxies suggest they occur on average about three times every century in the Milky Way, and that any galactic supernova would almost certainly be observable with modern astronomical telescopes.\n</p><p>Supernovae may expel much, if not all, of the material away from a star<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heger_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-heger-2">[2]</a></sup> at velocities up to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7007300000000000000&#9824;</span>30,000&#160;km/s</span> or 10% of the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. This drives an expanding and fast-moving <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> into the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>, and in turn, sweeping up an expanding shell of gas and dust, which is observed as a <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">supernova remnant</a>. Supernovae create, fuse and eject the bulk of the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> produced by <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Supernovae play a significant role in enriching the interstellar medium with the heavier <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic mass</a> chemical elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Furthermore, the expanding shock waves from supernovae can trigger the <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">formation of new stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa128_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-aaa128-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Supernova remnants are expected to accelerate a large fraction of galactic primary <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, but direct evidence for cosmic ray production was found only in a few of them so far.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ackermann-2013_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-ackermann-2013-8">[8]</a></sup> They are also potentially strong galactic sources of <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">gravitational waves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Theoretical studies indicate that most supernovae are triggered by one of two basic mechanisms: the sudden re-ignition of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Compact_star" title="Compact star">degenerate star</a> or the sudden <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">gravitational collapse</a> of a massive star\'s <a href="/wiki/Stellar_core" title="Stellar core">core</a>. In the first instance, a degenerate <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> may accumulate sufficient material from a <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary companion</a>, either through <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> or via a merger, to raise its core temperature enough to trigger <a href="/wiki/Thermal_runaway" title="Thermal runaway">runaway</a> nuclear fusion, completely disrupting the star. In the second case, the core of a <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution#Massive_stars" title="Stellar evolution">massive star</a> may undergo sudden gravitational collapse, releasing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_potential_energy" title="Gravitational potential energy">gravitational potential energy</a> as a supernova. While some observed supernovae are more complex than these two simplified theories, the astrophysical collapse mechanics have been established and accepted by most astronomers for some time.\n</p><p>Due to the wide range of astrophysical consequences of these events, astronomers now deem supernova research, across the fields of stellar and galactic evolution, as an especially important area for investigation.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" title="History of supernova observation">History of supernova observation</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" title="Crab Nebula">Crab Nebula</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Pulsar_wind_nebula" title="Pulsar wind nebula">pulsar wind nebula</a> associated with the <a href="/wiki/SN_1054" title="SN 1054">1054 supernova</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_(SN_1054)_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_(%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1046" data-file-width="1337" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_%28SN_1054%29_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_%28%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE%29.jpg/200px-Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_%28SN_1054%29_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_%28%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_%28SN_1054%29_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_%28%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE%29.jpg/300px-Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_%28SN_1054%29_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_%28%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_%28SN_1054%29_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_%28%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE%29.jpg/400px-Chinese_report_of_guest_star_identified_as_the_supernova_of_1054_%28SN_1054%29_in_the_Lidai_mingchen_zouyi_%28%E5%8E%86%E4%BB%A3%E5%90%8D%E8%87%A3%E5%A5%8F%E8%AE%AE%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The highlighted passages refer to the Chinese observation of SN 1054</div></div></div></div>\n<p>The earliest recorded supernova HB9 was viewed by <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indians</a> roughly 5,000-years ago and recorded in the oldest <a href="/wiki/Star_chart" title="Star chart">Star chart</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>. Later, the <a href="/wiki/SN_185" title="SN 185">SN 185</a>, was viewed by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">Chinese astronomers</a> in 185 AD. The brightest recorded supernova was <a href="/wiki/SN_1006" title="SN 1006">SN 1006</a>, which occurred in 1006 AD and was described by observers across China, Japan, Iraq, Egypt, and Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murdin_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murdin-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winkler_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-winkler-13">[13]</a></sup> The widely observed supernova <a href="/wiki/SN_1054" title="SN 1054">SN 1054</a> produced the <a href="/wiki/Crab_Nebula" title="Crab Nebula">Crab Nebula</a>. Supernovae <a href="/wiki/SN_1572" title="SN 1572">SN 1572</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SN_1604" title="SN 1604">SN 1604</a>, the latest to be observed with the naked eye in the Milky Way galaxy, had notable effects on the development of astronomy in Europe because they were used to argue against the <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotelian</a> idea that the universe beyond the Moon and planets was static and unchanging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> began observing SN 1604 at its peak on October 17, 1604, and continued to make estimates of its brightness until it faded from naked eye view a year later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kepler_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-kepler-15">[15]</a></sup> It was the second supernova to be observed in a generation (after SN 1572 seen by <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> in Cassiopeia).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is some evidence that the youngest galactic supernova, <a href="/wiki/G1.9%2B0.3" title="G1.9+0.3">G1.9+0.3</a>, occurred in the late 19th century, considerably more recently than <a href="/wiki/Cassiopeia_A" title="Cassiopeia A">Cassiopeia A</a> from around 1680.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chakraborti_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-chakraborti-17">[17]</a></sup> Neither supernova was noted at the time. In the case of G1.9+0.3, high extinction along the plane of the galaxy could have dimmed the event sufficiently to go unnoticed. The situation for Cassiopeia A is less clear. Infrared <a href="/wiki/Light_echo" title="Light echo">light echos</a> have been detected showing that it was a type IIb supernova and was not in a region of especially high <a href="/wiki/Extinction_(astronomy)" title="Extinction (astronomy)">extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-krause_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-krause-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Before the development of the <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>, only five supernovae were seen in the last <a href="/wiki/Millennium" title="Millennium">millennium</a>. Compared to a star\'s entire history, the visual appearance of a galactic supernova is very brief, perhaps spanning several months, so that the chances of observing one is roughly once in a lifetime. Only a tiny fraction of the 100 billion stars in a typical <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a> have the capacity to become a supernova, restricted to either those having large mass or extraordinarily rare kinds of <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary stars</a> containing <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarfs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, observation and discovery of extragalactic supernovae are now far more common. The first such observation was of <a href="/wiki/SN_1885A" title="SN 1885A">SN 1885A</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andromeda_galaxy" title="Andromeda galaxy">Andromeda galaxy</a>. Today, amateur and professional astronomers are finding several hundred every year, some when near maximum brightness, others on old astronomical photographs or plates. American astronomers <a href="/wiki/Rudolph_Minkowski" title="Rudolph Minkowski">Rudolph Minkowski</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Fritz Zwicky</a> developed the modern supernova classification scheme beginning in 1941.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> During the 1960s, astronomers found that the maximum intensities of supernovae could be used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_candles" title="Standard candles">standard candles</a>, hence indicators of astronomical distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Some of the most distant supernovae observed in 2003, appeared dimmer than expected. This supports the view that the expansion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerating_universe" title="Accelerating universe">universe is accelerating</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leibundgut_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leibundgut-22">[22]</a></sup> Techniques were developed for reconstructing supernovae events that have no written records of being observed. The date of the <a href="/wiki/Cassiopeia_A" title="Cassiopeia A">Cassiopeia A</a> supernova event was determined from <a href="/wiki/Light_echo" title="Light echo">light echoes</a> off <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> while the age of supernova remnant <a href="/wiki/RX_J0852.0-4622" title="RX J0852.0-4622">RX J0852.0-4622</a> was estimated from temperature measurements<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a> emissions from the radioactive decay of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanium-44" title="Titanium-44">titanium-44</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:One_galaxy,_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3679" data-file-width="4247" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/One_galaxy%2C_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg/220px-One_galaxy%2C_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/One_galaxy%2C_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg/330px-One_galaxy%2C_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/One_galaxy%2C_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg/440px-One_galaxy%2C_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:One_galaxy,_three_supernovae_RXC_J0949.8%2B1707.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>SN Antikythera, SN Eleanor and SN Alexander at galaxy cluster RXC J0949.8+1707.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The most luminous supernova ever recorded is <a href="/wiki/ASASSN-15lh" title="ASASSN-15lh">ASASSN-15lh</a>. It was first detected in June 2015 and peaked at 570 billion&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_luminosity" title="Solar luminosity"><var>L</var><sub>&#9737;</sub></a>, which is twice the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolometric_luminosity" title="Bolometric luminosity">bolometric luminosity</a> of any other known supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> However, the nature of this supernova continues to be debated and several alternative explanations have been suggested, e.g. tidal disruption of a star by a black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Among the earliest detected since time of detonation, and for which the earliest spectra have been obtained (beginning at 6 hours after the actual explosion), is the Type II <a href="/wiki/SN_2013fs" title="SN 2013fs">SN 2013fs</a> (iPTF13dqy) which was recorded 3 hours after the supernova event on 6 October 2013 by the <a href="/wiki/Palomar_Transient_Factory" title="Palomar Transient Factory">Intermediate Palomar Transient Factory</a> (iPTF). The star is located in a <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxy" title="Spiral galaxy">spiral galaxy</a> named <a href="/wiki/NGC_7610" title="NGC 7610">NGC 7610</a>, 160 million light years away in the constellation of Pegasus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 20 September 2016, amateur astronomer Victor Buso from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rosario" title="Rosario">Rosario</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> was testing out his new 16 inch telescope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AS_Now_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-AS_Now-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatBuso_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatBuso-32">[32]</a></sup> When taking several twenty second exposures of galaxy <a href="/wiki/NGC_613" title="NGC 613">NGC 613</a>, Buso chanced upon a supernova that had just become visible on earth. After examining the images he contacted the Instituto de Astrof&#237;sica de La Plata. "It was the first time anyone had ever captured the initial moments of the &#8220;shock breakout&#8221; from an optical supernova, one not associated with a gamma-ray or X-ray burst."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AS_Now_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-AS_Now-31">[31]</a></sup> The odds of capturing such an event were put between one in ten million to one in a hundred million, according to astronomer Melina Bersten from the Instituto de Astrof&#237;sica.\nThe supernova Buso observed was a Type IIb made by a star twenty times the mass of the  sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AS_Now_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-AS_Now-31">[31]</a></sup> Astronomer <a href="/wiki/Alex_Filippenko" title="Alex Filippenko">Alex Filippenko</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California" title="University of California">University of California</a>, remarked that professional astronomers had been searching for such an event for a long time. He stated: "Observations of stars in the first moments they begin exploding provide information that cannot be directly obtained in any other way."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AS_Now_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-AS_Now-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_supernova_observation#Telescope_observation" title="History of supernova observation">History of supernova observation &#167;&#160;Telescope observation</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3600" data-file-width="3600" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg/220px-NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg/330px-NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg/440px-NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NASA-SNR0519690-ChandraXRayObservatory-20150122.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">supernova remnant</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Early work on what was originally believed to be simply a new category of <a href="/wiki/Nova" title="Nova">novae</a> was performed during the 1930s by two astronomers named <a href="/wiki/Walter_Baade" title="Walter Baade">Walter Baade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Fritz Zwicky</a> at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wilson_Observatory" title="Mount Wilson Observatory">Mount Wilson Observatory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baade1934_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baade1934-33">[33]</a></sup> The name <i>super-novae</i> was first used during 1931 lectures held at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caltech" title="Caltech">Caltech</a> by Baade and Zwicky, then used publicly in 1933 at a meeting of the <a href="/wiki/American_Physical_Society" title="American Physical Society">American Physical Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baas33_1330_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-baas33_1330-1">[1]</a></sup> By 1938, the hyphen had been lost and the modern name was in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Because supernovae are relatively rare events within a galaxy, occurring about three times a century in the Milky Way,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reynolds_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-reynolds-35">[35]</a></sup> obtaining a good sample of supernovae to study requires regular monitoring of many galaxies.\n</p><p>Supernovae in other galaxies cannot be predicted with any meaningful accuracy. Normally, when they are discovered, they are already in progress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Most scientific interest in supernovae&#8212;as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_candle" title="Standard candle">standard candles</a> for measuring distance, for example&#8212;require an observation of their peak luminosity. It is therefore important to discover them well before they reach their maximum. <a href="/wiki/Amateur_astronomy" title="Amateur astronomy">Amateur astronomers</a>, who greatly outnumber professional astronomers, have played an important role in finding supernovae, typically by looking at some of the closer galaxies through an <a href="/wiki/Optical_telescope" title="Optical telescope">optical telescope</a> and comparing them to earlier photographs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Toward the end of the 20th century astronomers increasingly turned to computer-controlled telescopes and <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCDs</a> for hunting supernovae. While such systems are popular with amateurs, there are also professional installations such as the <a href="/wiki/Katzman_Automatic_Imaging_Telescope" title="Katzman Automatic Imaging Telescope">Katzman Automatic Imaging Telescope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Supernova_Early_Warning_System" title="Supernova Early Warning System">Supernova Early Warning System</a> (SNEWS) project uses a network of <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_detector" title="Neutrino detector">neutrino detectors</a> to give early warning of a supernova in the Milky Way galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">Neutrinos</a> are <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">particles</a> that are produced in great quantities by a supernova,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and they are not significantly absorbed by the interstellar gas and dust of the galactic disk.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_star_set_to_explode.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2008" data-file-width="3955" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/A_star_set_to_explode.jpg/260px-A_star_set_to_explode.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/A_star_set_to_explode.jpg/390px-A_star_set_to_explode.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/A_star_set_to_explode.jpg/520px-A_star_set_to_explode.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_star_set_to_explode.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"A star set to explode", the SBW1 nebula surrounds a massive blue supergiant in the <a href="/wiki/Carina_Nebula" title="Carina Nebula">Carina Nebula</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Supernova searches fall into two classes: those focused on relatively nearby events and those looking farther away. Because of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">expansion of the universe</a>, the distance to a remote object with a known <a href="/wiki/Emission_spectrum" title="Emission spectrum">emission spectrum</a> can be estimated by measuring its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shift</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a>); on average, more-distant objects recede with greater velocity than those nearby, and so have a higher redshift. Thus the search is split between high redshift and low redshift, with the boundary falling around a redshift range of <i>z</i>=0.1&#8211;0.3<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>&#8212;where <i>z</i> is a dimensionless measure of the spectrum\'s frequency shift.\n</p><p>High redshift searches for supernovae usually involve the observation of supernova light curves. These are useful for standard or calibrated candles to generate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_diagram" title="Hubble diagram">Hubble diagrams</a> and make cosmological predictions. Supernova spectroscopy, used to study the physics and environments of supernovae, is more practical at low than at high redshift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Low redshift observations also anchor the low-distance end of the Hubble curve, which is a plot of distance versus redshift for visible galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> (See also <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble\'s law">Hubble\'s law</a>).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming_convention">Naming convention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Naming convention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Keplers_supernova.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4800" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Keplers_supernova.jpg/220px-Keplers_supernova.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Keplers_supernova.jpg/330px-Keplers_supernova.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Keplers_supernova.jpg/440px-Keplers_supernova.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Keplers_supernova.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Multiwavelength <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical" title="Optical">optical</a> compilation image of <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Kepler\'s</a> <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">supernova remnant</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SN_1604" title="SN 1604">SN 1604</a>.</div></div></div><p>Supernova discoveries are reported to the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Central_Bureau_for_Astronomical_Telegrams" title="Central Bureau for Astronomical Telegrams">Central Bureau for Astronomical Telegrams</a>, which sends out a circular with the name it assigns to that supernova. The name is the marker <i>SN</i> followed by the year of discovery, suffixed with a one or two-letter designation. The first 26 supernovae of the year are designated with a capital letter from <i>A</i> to <i>Z</i>. Afterward pairs of lower-case letters are used: <i>aa</i>, <i>ab</i>, and so on. Hence, for example, <i>SN 2003C</i> designates the third supernova reported in the year 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The last supernova of 2005 was SN 2005nc, indicating that it was the 367th<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[nb 1]</a></sup> supernova found in 2005. Since 2000, professional and amateur astronomers have been finding several hundreds of supernovae each year (572 in 2007, 261 in 2008, 390 in 2009; 231 in 2013).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historical supernovae are known simply by the year they occurred: <a href="/wiki/SN_185" title="SN 185">SN 185</a>, <a href="/wiki/SN_1006" title="SN 1006">SN 1006</a>, <a href="/wiki/SN_1054" title="SN 1054">SN 1054</a>, <a href="/wiki/SN_1572" title="SN 1572">SN 1572</a> (called <i>Tycho\'s Nova</i>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SN_1604" title="SN 1604">SN 1604</a> (<i>Kepler\'s Star</i>). Since 1885 the additional letter notation has been used, even if there was only one supernova discovered that year (e.g. <a href="/wiki/SN_1885A" title="SN 1885A">SN 1885A</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SN_1907A&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SN 1907A (page does not exist)">SN 1907A</a>, etc.) &#8212; this last happened with <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SN_1947A&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SN 1947A (page does not exist)">SN 1947A</a>. <i>SN</i>, for SuperNova, is a standard prefix. Until 1987, two-letter designations were rarely needed; since 1988, however, they have been needed every year.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg/220px-Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg/330px-Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg/440px-Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_of_supernova_1993J.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s impression of supernova 1993J.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>As part of the attempt to understand supernovae, astronomers have classified them according to their <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curves</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absorption_line" title="Absorption line">absorption lines</a> of different <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> that appear in their <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy" title="Astronomical spectroscopy">spectra</a>. The first element for division is the presence or absence of a line caused by <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>. If a supernova\'s spectrum contains lines of hydrogen (known as the <a href="/wiki/Balmer_series" title="Balmer series">Balmer series</a> in the visual portion of the spectrum) it is classified <i>Type II</i>; otherwise it is <i>Type I</i>. In each of these two types there are subdivisions according to the presence of lines from other elements or the shape of the <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curve</a> (a graph of the supernova\'s <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> as a function of time).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-types_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-types-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taxonomy_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-taxonomy-53">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Supernova taxonomy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-types_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-types-52">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taxonomy_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-taxonomy-53">[52]</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<td rowspan="3" style="background:#ccc; valign:center;">Type I<br/>No hydrogen\n</td>\n<td colspan="4"><a href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernova" title="Type Ia supernova">Type Ia</a><br/>Presents a singly <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionized</a> <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> (Si II) line at 615.0 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a> (nanometers), near peak light\n</td>\n<td colspan="4" style="background:#fff;"><a href="/wiki/Supernova#Thermal_runaway" title="Supernova">Thermal runaway</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="2" style="background:#eee; valign:center;"><a href="/wiki/Type_Ib_and_Ic_supernovae" title="Type Ib and Ic supernovae">Type Ib/c</a><br/>Weak or no silicon absorption feature\n</td>\n<td colspan="3">Type Ib<br/>Shows a non-ionized <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> (He I) line at 587.6&#160;nm\n</td>\n<td rowspan="6" style="background:#fff;"><a href="/wiki/Supernova#Core_collapse" title="Supernova">Core collapse</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="3">Type Ic<br/>Weak or no helium\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="4" style="background:#ccc; valign:center;"><a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova" title="Type II supernova">Type II</a><br/>Shows hydrogen\n</td>\n<td rowspan="3" style="background:#ddd; valign:center;"><a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova" title="Type II supernova">Type II-P/L/N</a><br/>Type II spectrum throughout\n</td>\n<td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="background:#eee; valign:center;"><a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova#Light_curves_for_Type_II-L_and_Type_II-P_supernovae" title="Type II supernova">Type II-P/L</a><br/>No narrow lines\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova#Light_curves_for_Type_II-L_and_Type_II-P_supernovae" title="Type II supernova">Type II-P</a><br/>Reaches a "plateau" in its light curve\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova#Light_curves_for_Type_II-L_and_Type_II-P_supernovae" title="Type II supernova">Type II-L</a><br/>Displays a "linear" decrease in its light curve (linear in magnitude versus time).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doggett_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-doggett-54">[53]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="3"><a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova#Type_IIn_supernovae" title="Type II supernova">Type IIn</a><br/>Some narrow lines\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="4"><a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova#Type_IIb_supernovae" title="Type II supernova">Type IIb</a><br/>Spectrum changes to become like Type Ib\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Type_I">Type I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Type I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Type I supernovae are subdivided on the basis of their spectra, with Type Ia showing a strong <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ionised_silicon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ionised silicon (page does not exist)">ionised silicon</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absorption_line" title="Absorption line">absorption line</a>. Type I supernovae without this strong line are classified as Type Ib and Ic, with Type Ib showing strong neutral helium lines and Type Ic lacking them. The light curves are all similar, although Type Ia are generally brighter at peak luminosity, but the light curve is not important for classification of Type I supernovae.\n</p><p>A small number of Type Ia supernovae exhibit unusual features such as non-standard luminosity or broadened light curves, and these are typically classified by referring to the earliest example showing similar features. For example, the sub-luminous <a href="/wiki/SN_2008ha" title="SN 2008ha">SN 2008ha</a> is often referred to as <a href="/wiki/SN_2002cx" title="SN 2002cx">SN 2002cx</a>-like or class Ia-2002cx.\n</p><p>A small proportion of type Ic supernovae show highly broadened and blended emission lines which are taken to indicate very high expansion velocities for the ejecta. These have been classified as type Ic-BL or Ic-bl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bianco_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-bianco-55">[54]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Type_II">Type II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Type II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SNIIcurva.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1052" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/SNIIcurva.svg/220px-SNIIcurva.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/SNIIcurva.svg/330px-SNIIcurva.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/SNIIcurva.svg/440px-SNIIcurva.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SNIIcurva.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Light curves are used to classify Type II-P and Type II-L supernovae</div></div></div>\n<p>The supernovae of Type II can also be sub-divided based on their spectra. While most Type II supernovae show very broad <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emission_line" title="Emission line">emission lines</a> which indicate expansion velocities of many thousands of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilometres_per_second" title="Kilometres per second">kilometres per second</a>, some, such as <a href="/wiki/SN_2005gl" title="SN 2005gl">SN 2005gl</a>, have relatively narrow features in their spectra. These are called Type IIn, where the \'n\' stands for \'narrow\'.\n</p><p>A few supernovae, such as <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SN_1987K&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SN 1987K (page does not exist)">SN 1987K</a> and <a href="/wiki/SN_1993J" title="SN 1993J">SN 1993J</a>, appear to change types: they show lines of hydrogen at early times, but, over a period of weeks to months, become dominated by lines of helium. The term <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_IIb_supernova" title="Type IIb supernova">"Type IIb"</a> is used to describe the combination of features normally associated with Types II and Ib.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taxonomy_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-taxonomy-53">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Type II supernovae with normal spectra dominated by broad hydrogen lines that remain for the life of the decline are classified on the basis of their light curves. The most common type shows a distinctive "plateau" in the light curve shortly after peak brightness where the visual luminosity stays relatively constant for several months before the decline resumes. These are called Type II-P referring to the plateau. Less common are Type II-L supernovae that lack a distinct plateau. The "L" signifies "linear" although the light curve is not actually a straight line.\n</p><p>Supernovae that do not fit into the normal classifications are designated peculiar, or \'pec\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taxonomy_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-taxonomy-53">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Types_III.2C_IV.2C_and_V"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_III,_IV,_and_V">Types III, IV, and V</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Types III, IV, and V">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Fritz_Zwicky" title="Fritz Zwicky">Fritz Zwicky</a> defined additional supernovae types, although based on a very few examples that did not cleanly fit the parameters for a Type I or Type II supernova. <a href="/wiki/SN_1961i" title="SN 1961i">SN 1961i</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NGC_4303" title="NGC 4303">NGC 4303</a> was the prototype and only member of the Type III supernova class, noted for its broad light curve maximum and broad hydrogen Balmer lines that were slow to develop in the spectrum. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SN_1961f&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SN 1961f (page does not exist)">SN 1961f</a> in <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=NGC_3003&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="NGC 3003 (page does not exist)">NGC 3003</a> was the prototype and only member of the Type IV class, with a light curve similar to a Type II-P supernova, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_absorption_line" title="Hydrogen absorption line">hydrogen absorption lines</a> but weak <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_emission_line" title="Hydrogen emission line">hydrogen emission lines</a>. The Type V class was coined for <a href="/wiki/SN_1961V" title="SN 1961V">SN 1961V</a> in <a href="/wiki/NGC_1058" title="NGC 1058">NGC 1058</a>, an unusual faint supernova or <a href="/wiki/Supernova_impostor" title="Supernova impostor">supernova impostor</a> with a slow rise to brightness, a maximum lasting many months, and an unusual emission spectrum. The similarity of SN 1961V to the <a href="/wiki/Eta_Carinae" title="Eta Carinae">Eta Carinae</a> Great Outburst was noted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zwicky2_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-zwicky2-56">[55]</a></sup> Supernovae in M101 (1909) and M83 (1923 and 1957) were also suggested as possible Type IV or Type V supernovae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zwicky_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-zwicky-57">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>These types would now all be treated as peculiar Type II supernovae, of which many more examples have been discovered, although it is still debated whether SN 1961V was a true supernova following an <a href="/wiki/Luminous_blue_variable" title="Luminous blue variable">LBV</a> outburst or an impostor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doggett_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-doggett-54">[53]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_models">Current models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Current models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="1580" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg/260px-The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg/390px-The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg/520px-The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Rise_and_Fall_of_a_Supernova.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sequence shows the rapid brightening and slower fading of a supernova in the galaxy <a href="/wiki/NGC_1365" title="NGC 1365">NGC 1365</a> (the bright dot close to the upper part of the galactic center)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The type codes, described above given to supernovae, are <i><a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(general)" title="Taxonomy (general)">taxonomic</a></i> in nature: the type number describes the light observed from the supernova, not necessarily its cause. For example, Type Ia supernovae are produced by runaway fusion ignited on degenerate <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> progenitors while the spectrally similar Type Ib/c are produced from massive Wolf&#8211;Rayet progenitors by core collapse. The following summarizes what is currently believed to be the most plausible explanations for supernovae.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermal_runaway">Thermal runaway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Thermal runaway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Type_Ia_supernova" title="Type Ia supernova">Type Ia supernova</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="761" data-file-width="742" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg/220px-Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg/330px-Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg/440px-Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Progenitor_IA_supernova.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Formation of a Type Ia supernova</div></div></div>\n<p>A white dwarf star may accumulate sufficient material from a <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">stellar companion</a> to raise its core temperature enough to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_detonation" title="Carbon detonation">ignite</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_burning_process" title="Carbon burning process">carbon fusion</a>, at which point it undergoes <a href="/wiki/Thermal_runaway" title="Thermal runaway">runaway</a> nuclear fusion, completely disrupting it. There are three avenues by which this detonation is theorized to happen: stable <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> of material from a companion, the collision of two white dwarfs, or accretion that causes ignition in a shell that then ignites. The dominant mechanism by which Type Ia supernovae are produced remains unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piro2014_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piro2014-59">[58]</a></sup> Despite this uncertainty in how Type Ia supernovae are produced, Type Ia supernovae have very uniform properties, and are useful standard candles over intergalactic distances. Some calibrations are required to compensate for the gradual change in properties or different frequencies of abnormal luminosity supernovae at high red shift, and for small variations in brightness identified by light curve shape or spectrum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chen_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-chen-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Normal_Type_Ia">Normal Type Ia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Normal Type Ia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>There are several means by which a supernova of this type can form, but they share a common underlying mechanism. If a <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>-<a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[nb 2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> accreted enough matter to reach the <a href="/wiki/Chandrasekhar_limit" title="Chandrasekhar limit">Chandrasekhar limit</a> of about 1.44 <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a> (<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub></a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mazzali2007_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mazzali2007-63">[61]</a></sup> (for a non-rotating star), it would no longer be able to support the bulk of its mass through <a href="/wiki/Electron_degeneracy_pressure" title="Electron degeneracy pressure">electron degeneracy pressure</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chandrasekhar_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chandrasekhar-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> and would begin to collapse. However, the current view is that this limit is not normally attained; increasing temperature and density inside the core <a href="/wiki/Carbon_detonation" title="Carbon detonation">ignite</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_burning_process" title="Carbon burning process">carbon fusion</a> as the star approaches the limit (to within about 1%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup>), before collapse is initiated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mazzali2007_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mazzali2007-63">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Within a few seconds, a substantial fraction of the matter in the white dwarf undergoes nuclear fusion, releasing enough energy (1&#8211;<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7044200000000000000&#9824;</span>2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>44</sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">J</a></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa270_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-aaa270-67">[65]</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_binding_energy" title="Gravitational binding energy">unbind</a> the star in a supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ropke_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-ropke-68">[66]</a></sup> An outwardly expanding <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a> is generated, with matter reaching velocities on the order of 5,000&#8211;20,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilometers_per_second" title="Kilometers per second">km/s</a>, or roughly 3% of the speed of light. There is also a significant increase in luminosity, reaching an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> of &#8722;19.3 (or 5 billion times brighter than the Sun), with little variation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-explosion_model_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-explosion_model-69">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The model for the formation of this category of supernova is a closed <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary star</a> system. The larger of the two stars is the first to <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">evolve</a> off the <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a>, and it expands to form a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a>. The two stars now share a common envelope, causing their mutual orbit to shrink. The giant star then sheds most of its envelope, losing mass until it can no longer continue <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>. At this point it becomes a white dwarf star, composed primarily of carbon and oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup> Eventually the secondary star also evolves off the main sequence to form a red giant. Matter from the giant is accreted by the white dwarf, causing the latter to increase in mass. Despite widespread acceptance of the basic model, the exact details of initiation and of the heavy elements produced in the catastrophic event are still unclear.\n</p><p>Type Ia supernovae follow a characteristic <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curve</a>&#8212;the graph of luminosity as a function of time&#8212;after the event. This luminosity is generated by the <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>-56 through <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>-56 to <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>-56.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-explosion_model_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-explosion_model-69">[67]</a></sup> The peak luminosity of the light curve is extremely consistent across normal Type Ia supernovae, having a maximum <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> of about &#8722;19.3. This allows them to be used as a secondary<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_candle" title="Standard candle">standard candle</a> to measure the distance to their host galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-standard_Type_Ia">Non-standard Type Ia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Non-standard Type Ia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Another model for the formation of Type Ia supernovae involves the merger of two white dwarf stars, with the combined mass momentarily exceeding the <a href="/wiki/Chandrasekhar_limit" title="Chandrasekhar limit">Chandrasekhar limit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> There is much variation in this type of event,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> and in many cases there may be no supernova at all, but it is expected that they will have a broader and less luminous light curve than the more normal SN Type Ia.\n</p><p>Abnormally bright Type Ia supernovae are expected when the white dwarf already has a mass higher than the Chandrasekhar limit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> possibly enhanced further by asymmetry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> but the ejected material will have less than normal kinetic energy.\n</p><p>There is no formal sub-classification for the non-standard Type Ia supernovae. It has been proposed that a group of sub-luminous supernovae that occur when helium accretes onto a white dwarf should be classified as <b>Type Iax</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wang_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-wang-77">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foley_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-foley-78">[76]</a></sup> This type of supernova may not always completely destroy the white dwarf progenitor and could leave behind a <a href="/wiki/Zombie_star" title="Zombie star">zombie star</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccully_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-mccully-79">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One specific type of non-standard Type Ia supernova develops hydrogen, and other, emission lines and gives the appearance of mixture between a normal Type Ia and a Type IIn supernova. Examples are <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SN_2002ic&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SN 2002ic (page does not exist)">SN 2002ic</a> and <a href="/wiki/SN_2005gj" title="SN 2005gj">SN 2005gj</a>. These supernova have been dubbed <b>Type Ia/IIn</b>, <b>Type Ian</b>, <b>Type IIa</b> and <b>Type IIan</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Core_collapse">Core collapse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Core collapse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Supernovae_as_initial_mass-metallicity.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Supernovae_as_initial_mass-metallicity.svg/260px-Supernovae_as_initial_mass-metallicity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Supernovae_as_initial_mass-metallicity.svg/390px-Supernovae_as_initial_mass-metallicity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Supernovae_as_initial_mass-metallicity.svg/520px-Supernovae_as_initial_mass-metallicity.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Supernovae_as_initial_mass-metallicity.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Supernova types by initial mass-metallicity</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg/220px-Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg/330px-Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg/440px-Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Evolved_star_fusion_shells.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The layers of a massive, evolved star just prior to core collapse (Not to scale)</div></div></div>\n<p>Very massive stars can undergo core collapse when nuclear fusion becomes unable to sustain the core against its own gravity; passing this threshold is the cause of all types of supernova except Type Ia. The collapse may cause violent expulsion of the outer layers of the star resulting in a supernova, or the release of gravitational potential energy may be insufficient and the star may collapse into a <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a> with little radiated energy.\n</p><p>Core collapse can be caused by several different mechanisms: <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a>; exceeding the <a href="/wiki/Chandrasekhar_limit" title="Chandrasekhar limit">Chandrasekhar limit</a>; <a href="/wiki/Pair-instability_supernova" title="Pair-instability supernova">pair-instability</a>; or <a href="/wiki/Photodisintegration" title="Photodisintegration">photodisintegration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heger_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-heger-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nomoto_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-nomoto-81">[79]</a></sup> When a massive star develops an iron core larger than the Chandrasekhar mass it will no longer be able to support itself by <a href="/wiki/Electron_degeneracy_pressure" title="Electron degeneracy pressure">electron degeneracy pressure</a> and will collapse further to a neutron star or black hole. Electron capture by magnesium in a degenerate O/Ne/Mg core causes <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">gravitational collapse</a> followed by explosive oxygen fusion, with very similar results. Electron-positron pair production in a large post-helium burning core removes thermodynamic support and causes initial collapse followed by runaway fusion, resulting in a pair-instability supernova. A sufficiently large and hot <a href="/wiki/Stellar_core" title="Stellar core">stellar core</a> may generate gamma-rays energetic enough to initiate photodisintegration directly, which will cause a complete collapse of the core.\n</p><p>The table below lists the known reasons for core collapse in massive stars, the types of star that they occur in, their associated supernova type, and the remnant produced. The <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a> is the proportion of elements other than hydrogen or helium, as compared to the Sun. The initial mass is the mass of the star prior to the supernova event, given in multiples of the Sun\'s mass, although the mass at the time of the supernova may be much lower.\n</p><p>Type IIn supernovae are not listed in the table. They can potentially be produced by various types of core collapse in different progenitor stars, possibly even by Type Ia white dwarf ignitions, although it seems that most will be from iron core collapse in luminous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supergiant" title="Supergiant">supergiants</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hypergiant" title="Hypergiant">hypergiants</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Luminous_blue_variable" title="Luminous blue variable">LBVs</a>). The narrow spectral lines for which they are named occur because the supernova is expanding into a small dense cloud of circumstellar material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moriya_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-moriya-82">[80]</a></sup> It appears that a significant proportion of supposed Type IIn supernovae are actually <a href="/wiki/Supernova_impostor" title="Supernova impostor">supernova impostors</a>, massive eruptions of <a href="/wiki/Luminous_blue_variable" title="Luminous blue variable">LBV</a>-like stars similar to the Great Eruption of <a href="/wiki/Eta_Carinae" title="Eta Carinae">Eta Carinae</a>. In these events, material previously ejected from the star creates the narrow absorption lines and causes a shock wave through interaction with the newly ejected material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith-83">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Core collapse scenarios by mass and metallicity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heger_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-heger-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Cause of collapse</th>\n<th>Progenitor star approximate initial mass (<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a>)</th>\n<th>Supernova type</th>\n<th>Remnant\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Electron capture in a degenerate O+Ne+Mg core</td>\n<td>8&#8211;10</td>\n<td>Faint II-P</td>\n<td>Neutron star\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td rowspan="7">Iron core collapse</td>\n<td>10&#8211;25</td>\n<td>Faint II-P</td>\n<td>Neutron star\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>25&#8211;40 with low or solar metallicity</td>\n<td>Normal II-P</td>\n<td>Black hole after fallback of material onto an initial neutron star\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>25&#8211;40 with very high metallicity</td>\n<td>II-L or II-b</td>\n<td>Neutron star\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>40&#8211;90 with low metallicity</td>\n<td>None</td>\n<td>Black hole\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>&#8805;40 with near-solar metallicity</td>\n<td>Faint Ib/c, or hypernova with <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst" title="Gamma-ray burst">gamma-ray burst</a> (GRB)</td>\n<td>Black hole after fallback of material onto an initial neutron star\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>&#8805;40 with very high metallicity</td>\n<td>Ib/c</td>\n<td>Neutron star\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>&#8805;90 with low metallicity</td>\n<td>None, possible GRB</td>\n<td>Black hole\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Pair instability</td>\n<td>140&#8211;250 with low metallicity</td>\n<td>II-P, sometimes a hypernova, possible GRB</td>\n<td>No remnant\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Photodisintegration</td>\n<td>&#8805;250 with low metallicity</td>\n<td>None (or luminous supernova?), possible GRB</td>\n<td>Massive black hole\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Remnants_of_single_massive_stars.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="315" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Remnants_of_single_massive_stars.svg/260px-Remnants_of_single_massive_stars.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Remnants_of_single_massive_stars.svg/390px-Remnants_of_single_massive_stars.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Remnants_of_single_massive_stars.svg/520px-Remnants_of_single_massive_stars.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Remnants_of_single_massive_stars.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Remnants of single massive stars</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Core_collapse_scenario.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="775" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Core_collapse_scenario.svg/310px-Core_collapse_scenario.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Core_collapse_scenario.svg/465px-Core_collapse_scenario.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Core_collapse_scenario.svg/620px-Core_collapse_scenario.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Core_collapse_scenario.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Within a massive, evolved star (a) the onion-layered shells of elements undergo fusion, forming an iron core (b) that reaches Chandrasekhar-mass and starts to collapse. The inner part of the core is compressed into neutrons (c), causing infalling material to bounce (d) and form an outward-propagating shock front (red). The shock starts to stall (e), but it is re-invigorated by a process that may include neutrino interaction. The surrounding material is blasted away (f), leaving only a degenerate remnant.</div></div></div>\n<p>When a stellar core is no longer supported against gravity, it collapses in on itself with velocities reaching 70,000&#160;km/s (0.23<a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light"><i>c</i></a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grav_waves_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-grav_waves-84">[82]</a></sup> resulting in a rapid increase in temperature and density. What follows next depends on the mass and structure of the collapsing core, with low mass degenerate cores forming neutron stars, higher mass degenerate cores mostly collapsing completely to black holes, and non-degenerate cores undergoing runaway fusion.\n</p><p>The initial collapse of degenerate cores is accelerated by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>, photodisintegration and electron capture, which causes a burst of <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino">electron neutrinos</a>. As the density increases, neutrino emission is cut off as they become trapped in the core. The inner core eventually reaches typically 30&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilometer" title="Kilometer">km</a> diameter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WoosleyJanka_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-WoosleyJanka-85">[83]</a></sup> and a density comparable to that of an <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nucleus</a>, and neutron <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degeneracy_pressure" title="Degeneracy pressure">degeneracy pressure</a> tries to halt the collapse. If the core mass is more than about 15&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub></a> then neutron degeneracy is insufficient to stop the collapse and a black hole forms directly with no supernova.\n</p><p>In lower mass cores the collapse is stopped and the newly formed neutron core has an initial temperature of about 100 billion <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a>, 6000 times the temperature of the sun\'s core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> At this temperature, neutrino-antineutrino pairs of all <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_oscillation" title="Neutrino oscillation">flavors</a> are efficiently formed by <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">thermal emission</a>. These thermal neutrinos are several times more abundant than the electron-capture neutrinos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup> About 10<sup>46</sup> joules, approximately 10% of the star\'s rest mass, is converted into a ten-second burst of neutrinos which is the main output of the event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WoosleyJanka_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-WoosleyJanka-85">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barwick_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-barwick-88">[86]</a></sup> The suddenly halted core collapse rebounds and produces a <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a> that stalls within milliseconds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> in the outer core as energy is lost through the dissociation of heavy elements. A process that is not clearly understood<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> is necessary to allow the outer layers of the core to reabsorb around 10<sup>44</sup> joules<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barwick_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-barwick-88">[86]</a></sup> (1 <a href="/wiki/Foe_(unit)" title="Foe (unit)">foe</a>) from the neutrino pulse, producing the visible brightness, although there are also other theories on how to power the explosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WoosleyJanka_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-WoosleyJanka-85">[83]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some material from the outer envelope falls back onto the neutron star, and for cores beyond about 8&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub> there is sufficient fallback to form a black hole. This fallback will reduce the kinetic energy created and the mass of expelled radioactive material, but in some situations it may also generate relativistic jets that result in a gamma-ray burst or an exceptionally luminous supernova.\n</p><p>Collapse of massive non-degenerate cores will ignite further fusion. When the core collapse is initiated by pair instability, oxygen fusion begins and the collapse may be halted. For core masses of 40&#8211;60&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>, the collapse halts and the star remains intact, but core collapse will occur again when a larger core has formed. For cores of around 60&#8211;130&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>, the fusion of oxygen and heavier elements is so energetic that the entire star is disrupted, causing a supernova. At the upper end of the mass range, the supernova is unusually luminous and extremely long-lived due to many solar masses of ejected <sup>56</sup>Ni. For even larger core masses, the core temperature becomes high enough to allow photodisintegration and the core collapses completely into a black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kasen_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-kasen-90">[88]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Type_II_2">Type II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Type II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova" title="Type II supernova">Type II supernova</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SN_1997D.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="399" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/SN_1997D.jpg/220px-SN_1997D.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/SN_1997D.jpg/330px-SN_1997D.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/74/SN_1997D.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SN_1997D.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The atypical subluminous Type II <a href="/wiki/SN_1997D" title="SN 1997D">SN 1997D</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Stars with initial masses less than about eight times the sun never develop a core large enough to collapse and they eventually lose their atmospheres to become white dwarfs. Stars with at least 9&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub> (possibly as much as 12&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-superagb_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-superagb-91">[89]</a></sup>) evolve in a complex fashion, progressively burning heavier elements at hotter temperatures in their cores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WoosleyJanka_85-3"><a href="#cite_note-WoosleyJanka-85">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science304_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-science304-92">[90]</a></sup> The star becomes layered like an onion, with the burning of more easily fused elements occurring in larger shells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heger_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-heger-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hinshaw_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-hinshaw-93">[91]</a></sup> Although popularly described as an onion with an iron core, the least massive supernova progenitors only have oxygen-neon(-magnesium) cores. These <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch#super-agb_stars" title="Asymptotic giant branch">super AGB</a> stars may form the majority of core collapse supernovae, although less luminous and so less commonly observed than those from more massive progenitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-superagb_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-superagb-91">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>If core collapse occurs during a supergiant phase when the star still has a hydrogen envelope, the result is a Type II supernova. The rate of mass loss for luminous stars depends on the metallicity and luminosity.
