Extremely luminous stars at near solar metallicity will lose all their hydrogen before they reach core collapse and so will not form a Type II supernova. At low metallicity, all stars will reach core collapse with a hydrogen envelope but sufficiently massive stars collapse directly to a black hole without producing a visible supernova.\n</p><p>Stars with an initial mass up to about 90 times the sun, or a little less at high metallicity, are expected to result in a Type II-P supernova which is the most commonly observed type. At moderate to high metallicity, stars near the upper end of that mass range will have lost most of their hydrogen when core collapse occurs and the result will be a Type II-L supernova. At very low metallicity, stars of around 140&#8211;250&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub> will reach core collapse by pair instability while they still have a hydrogen atmosphere and an oxygen core and the result will be a supernova with Type II characteristics but a very large mass of ejected <sup>56</sup>Ni and high luminosity.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Type_Ib_and_Ic">Type Ib and Ic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Type Ib and Ic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Type_Ib_and_Ic_supernovae" title="Type Ib and Ic supernovae">Type Ib and Ic supernovae</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Supernova_2008D.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="538" data-file-width="1082" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Supernova_2008D.jpg/260px-Supernova_2008D.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Supernova_2008D.jpg/390px-Supernova_2008D.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Supernova_2008D.jpg/520px-Supernova_2008D.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Supernova_2008D.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>SN 2008D, a Type Ib<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> supernova, shown in <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> (left) and visible light (right) at the far upper end of the galaxy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>These supernovae, like those of Type II, are massive stars that undergo core collapse. However the stars which become Types Ib and Ic supernovae have lost most of their outer (hydrogen) envelopes due to strong <a href="/wiki/Stellar_wind" title="Stellar wind">stellar winds</a> or else from interaction with a companion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup> These stars are known as <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf&#8211;Rayet star">Wolf&#8211;Rayet stars</a>, and they occur at moderate to high metallicity where continuum driven winds cause sufficiently high mass loss rates. Observations of Type Ib/c supernova do not match the observed or expected occurrence of Wolf&#8211;Rayet stars and alternate explanations for this type of core collapse supernova involve stars stripped of their hydrogen by binary interactions. Binary models provide a better match for the observed supernovae, with the proviso that no suitable binary helium stars have ever been observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eldridge_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-eldridge-97">[95]</a></sup> Since a supernova can occur whenever the mass of the star at the time of core collapse is low enough not to cause complete fallback to a black hole, any massive star may result in a supernova if it loses enough mass before core collapse occurs.\n</p><p>Type Ib supernovae are the more common and result from Wolf&#8211;Rayet stars of Type WC which still have helium in their atmospheres. For a narrow range of masses, stars evolve further before reaching core collapse to become WO stars with very little helium remaining and these are the progenitors of Type Ic supernovae.\n</p><p>A few percent of the Type Ic supernovae are associated with <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst" title="Gamma-ray burst">gamma-ray bursts</a> (GRB), though it is also believed that any hydrogen-stripped Type Ib or Ic supernova could produce a GRB, depending on the circumstances of the geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup> The mechanism for producing this type of GRB is the jets produced by the magnetic field of the rapidly spinning <a href="/wiki/Magnetar" title="Magnetar">magnetar</a> formed at the collapsing core of the star. The jets would also transfer energy into the expanding outer shell, producing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super-luminous_supernova" title="Super-luminous supernova">super-luminous supernova</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ultra-stripped supernovae occur when the exploding star has been stripped (almost) all the way to the metal core, via mass transfer in a close binary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup> As a result, very little material is ejected from the exploding star (c. 0.1&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub>). In the most extreme cases, ultra-stripped supernovae can occur in naked metal cores, barely above the Chandrasekhar mass limit. SN 2005ek<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup> might be an observational example of an ultra-stripped supernova, giving rise to a relatively dim and fast decaying light curve. The nature of ultra-stripped supernovae can be both iron core-collapse and electron capture supernovae, depending on the mass of the collapsing core.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Failed">Failed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Failed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Failed_supernova" title="Failed supernova">Failed supernova</a></div>\n<p>The core collapse of some massive stars may not result in a visible supernova. The main model for this is a sufficiently massive core that the kinetic energy is insufficient to reverse the infall of the outer layers onto a black hole. These events are difficult to detect, but large surveys have detected possible candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-failed_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-failed-103">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gerke_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-gerke-104">[102]</a></sup> The red supergiant <a href="/wiki/N6946-BH1" title="N6946-BH1">N6946-BH1</a> in <a href="/wiki/NGC_6946" title="NGC 6946">NGC 6946</a> underwent a modest outburst in March 2009, before fading from view. Only a faint <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> source remains at the star\'s location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-adams-105">[103]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Light_curves">Light curves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Light curves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1187" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png/220px-Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png/330px-Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png/440px-Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparative supernova type light curves</div></div></div>\n<p>A historic puzzle concerned the source of energy that can maintain the optical supernova glow for months. Although the energy that disrupts each type of supernovae is delivered promptly, the light curves are mostly dominated by subsequent radioactive heating of the rapidly expanding ejecta. Some have considered rotational energy from the central pulsar. The ejecta gases would dim quickly without some energy input to keep it hot. The intensely radioactive nature of the ejecta gases, which is now known to be correct for most supernovae, was first calculated on sound nucleosynthesis grounds in the late 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> It was not until <a href="/wiki/SN_1987A" title="SN 1987A">SN 1987A</a> that direct observation of gamma-ray lines unambiguously identified the major radioactive nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S.M._Matz,_G.H._Share_1988_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-S.M._Matz,_G.H._Share_1988-107">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is now known by direct observation that much of the <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curve</a> (the graph of luminosity as a function of time) after the occurrence of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_II_Supernova" title="Type II Supernova">Type II Supernova</a>, such as SN 1987A, is explained by those predicted <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decays</a>. Although the luminous emission consists of optical photons, it is the radioactive power absorbed by the ejected gases that keeps the remnant hot enough to radiate light. The <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> of <sup>56</sup><a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">Ni</a> through its daughters <sup>56</sup><a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">Co</a> to <sup>56</sup><a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">Fe</a> produces gamma-ray <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>, primarily of 847keV and 1238keV, that are absorbed and dominate the heating and thus the luminosity of the ejecta at intermediate times (several weeks) to late times (several months).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup> Energy for the peak of the light curve of SN1987A was provided by the decay of <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_nickel" title="Isotopes of nickel"><sup>56</sup>Ni</a> to <sup>56</sup>Co (half life 6 days) while energy for the later light curve in particular fit very closely with the 77.3 day half-life of <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_cobalt" title="Isotopes of cobalt"><sup>56</sup>Co</a> decaying to <sup>56</sup>Fe. Later measurements by space gamma-ray telescopes of the small fraction of the <sup>56</sup>Co and <sup>57</sup>Co gamma rays that escaped the <a href="/wiki/SN_1987A" title="SN 1987A">SN 1987A</a> remnant without absorption confirmed earlier predictions that those two radioactive nuclei were the power sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S.M._Matz,_G.H._Share_1988_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-S.M._Matz,_G.H._Share_1988-107">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The visual light curves of the different supernova types all depend at late times on radioactive heating, but they vary in shape and amplitude because of the underlying mechanisms, the way that visible radiation is produced, the epoch of its observation, and the transparency of the ejected material. The light curves can be significantly different at other wavelengths. For example, at ultraviolet wavelengths there is an early extremely luminous peak lasting only a few hours corresponding to the breakout of the shock launched by the initial event, but that breakout is hardly detectable optically.\n</p><p>The light curves for Type Ia are mostly very uniform, with a consistent maximum absolute magnitude and a relatively steep decline in luminosity. Their optical energy output is driven by radioactive decay of ejected nickel-56 (half life 6 days), which then decays to radioactive cobalt-56 (half life 77 days). These radioisotopes excite the surrounding material to incandescence. Studies of cosmology today rely on <sup>56</sup><a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">Ni</a> radioactivity providing the energy for the optical brightness of supernovae of Type Ia, which are the "standard candles" of cosmology but whose diagnostic 847keV and 1238keV gamma rays were first detected only in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup> The initial phases of the light curve decline steeply as the effective size of the photosphere decreases and trapped electromagnetic radiation is depleted. The light curve continues to decline in the B band while it may show a small shoulder in the visual at about 40 days, but this is only a hint of a secondary maximum that occurs in the infra-red as certain ionised heavy elements recombine to produce infra-red radiation and the ejecta become transparent to it. The visual light curve continues to decline at a rate slightly greater than the decay rate of the radioactive cobalt (which has the longer half life and controls the later curve), because the ejected material becomes more diffuse and less able to convert the high energy radiation into visual radiation. After several months, the light curve changes its decline rate again as <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">positron emission</a> becomes dominant from the remaining cobalt-56, although this portion of the light curve has been little-studied.\n</p><p>Type Ib and Ic light curves are basically similar to Type Ia although with a lower average peak luminosity. The visual light output is again due to radioactive decay being converted into visual radiation, but there is a much lower mass of the created nickel-56. The peak luminosity varies considerably and there are even occasional Type Ib/c supernovae orders of magnitude more and less luminous than the norm. The most luminous Type Ic supernovae are referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypernova" title="Hypernova">hypernovae</a> and tend to have broadened light curves in addition to the increased peak luminosity. The source of the extra energy is thought to be relativistic jets driven by the formation of a rotating black hole, which also produce <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst" title="Gamma-ray burst">gamma-ray bursts</a>.\n</p><p>The light curves for Type II supernovae are characterised by a much slower decline than Type I, on the order of 0.05 <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">magnitudes</a> per day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup> excluding the plateau phase. The visual light output is dominated by kinetic energy rather than radioactive decay for several months, due primarily to the existence of hydrogen in the ejecta from the atmosphere of the supergiant progenitor star. In the initial destruction this hydrogen becomes heated and ionised. The majority of Type II supernovae show a prolonged plateau in their light curves as this hydrogen recombines, emitting visible light and becoming more transparent. This is then followed by a declining light curve driven by radioactive decay although slower than in Type I supernovae, due to the efficiency of conversion into light by all the hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doggett_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-doggett-54">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Type II-L the plateau is absent because the progenitor had relatively little hydrogen left in its atmosphere, sufficient to appear in the spectrum but insufficient to produce a noticeable plateau in the light output. In Type IIb supernovae the hydrogen atmosphere of the progenitor is so depleted (thought to be due to tidal stripping by a companion star) that the light curve is closer to a Type I supernova and the hydrogen even disappears from the spectrum after several weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doggett_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-doggett-54">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Type IIn supernovae are characterised by additional narrow spectral lines produced in a dense shell of circumstellar material. Their light curves are generally very broad and extended, occasionally also extremely luminous and referred to as a superluminous supernova. These light curves are produced by the highly efficient conversion of kinetic energy of the ejecta into electromagnetic radiation by interaction with the dense shell of material. This only occurs when the material is sufficiently dense and compact, indicating that it has been produced by the progenitor star itself only shortly before the supernova occurs.\n</p><p>Large numbers of supernovae have been catalogued and classified to provide distance candles and test models. Average characteristics vary somewhat with distance and type of host galaxy, but can broadly be specified for each supernova type.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="max-width: 600px">\n<caption>Physical properties of supernovae by type<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weidong_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-weidong-111">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" (faint)</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">around &#8722;14</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">0.1</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">roughly 15</td>\n<td 15</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">Plateau then around 50\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>IIn<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_d"><a href="#endnote_d">d</a></sup></td>\n<td style="text-align:right">50&#8211;150\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>IIn (bright)</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">to &#8722;22</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">above 5</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">above 50</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">above 100\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Notes:\n</p>\n<div class="plainlist">\n<ul><li>a. <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_a"><b><a href="#ref_a">^</a></b></span> Faint types may be a distinct sub-class. Bright types may be a continuum from slightly over-luminous to hypernovae.</li>\n<li>b. <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_b"><b><a href="#ref_b">^</a></b></span> These magnitudes are measured in the R band. Measurements in V or B bands are common and will be around half a magnitude brighter for supernovae.</li>\n<li>c. <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_c"><b><a href="#ref_c">^</a></b></span> <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">Order of magnitude</a> kinetic energy. Total electromagnetic radiated energy is usually lower, (theoretical) neutrino energy much higher.</li>\n<li>d. <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_d"><b><a href="#ref_d">^</a></b></span> Probably a heterogeneous group, any of the other types embedded in nebulosity.</li></ul>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asymmetry">Asymmetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Asymmetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chandra-crab.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Chandra-crab.jpg/220px-Chandra-crab.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Chandra-crab.jpg/330px-Chandra-crab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Chandra-crab.jpg/440px-Chandra-crab.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chandra-crab.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">pulsar</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crab_nebula" title="Crab nebula">Crab nebula</a> is travelling at 375 km/s relative to the nebula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>A long-standing puzzle surrounding Type II supernovae is why the remaining compact object receives a large velocity away from the epicentre;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">pulsars</a>, and thus neutron stars, are observed to have high velocities, and black holes presumably do as well, although they are far harder to observe in isolation. The initial impetus can be substantial, propelling an object of more than a solar mass at a velocity of 500&#160;km/s or greater. This indicates an expansion asymmetry, but the mechanism by which momentum is transferred to the compact object remains<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> a puzzle. Proposed explanations for this kick include convection in the collapsing star and jet production during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_star_formation" title="Neutron star formation">neutron star formation</a>.\n</p><p>One possible explanation for this asymmetry is a large-scale <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> above the core. The convection can create variations in the local abundances of elements, resulting in uneven nuclear burning during the collapse, bounce and resulting expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another possible explanation is that accretion of gas onto the central neutron star can create a <a href="/wiki/Accretion_disk" title="Accretion disk">disk</a> that drives highly directional jets, propelling matter at a high velocity out of the star, and driving transverse shocks that completely disrupt the star. These jets might play a crucial role in the resulting supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup> (A similar model is now favored for explaining long <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst" title="Gamma-ray burst">gamma-ray bursts</a>.)\n</p><p>Initial asymmetries have also been confirmed in Type Ia supernovae through observation. This result may mean that the initial luminosity of this type of supernova depends on the viewing angle. However, the expansion becomes more symmetrical with the passage of time. Early asymmetries are detectable by measuring the polarization of the emitted light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_output">Energy output</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Energy output">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SNIacurva.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="214" data-file-width="299" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/SNIacurva.png/220px-SNIacurva.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/SNIacurva.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SNIacurva.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The radioactive decays of nickel-56 and cobalt-56 that produce a supernova visible light curve</div></div></div>\n<p>Although we are used to thinking of supernovae primarily as luminous visible events, the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> they release is almost a minor side-effect. Particularly in the case of core collapse supernovae, the emitted electromagnetic radiation is a tiny fraction of the total energy released during the event.\n</p><p>There is a fundamental difference between the balance of energy production in the different types of supernova. In Type Ia white dwarf detonations, most of the energy is directed into <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">heavy element synthesis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of the ejecta. In core collapse supernovae, the vast majority of the energy is directed into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supernova_neutrino" title="Supernova neutrino">neutrino</a> emission, and while some of this apparently powers the observed destruction, 99%+ of the neutrinos escape the star in the first few minutes following the start of the collapse.\n</p><p>Type Ia supernovae derive their energy from a runaway nuclear fusion of a carbon-oxygen white dwarf. The details of the energetics are still not fully understood, but the end result is the ejection of the entire mass of the original star at high kinetic energy. Around half a solar mass of that mass is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickel-56" title="Nickel-56"><sup>56</sup>Ni</a> generated from <a href="/wiki/Silicon-burning_process" title="Silicon-burning process">silicon burning</a>. <sup>56</sup>Ni is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> and decays into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cobalt-56" title="Cobalt-56"><sup>56</sup>Co</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta_plus_decay" title="Beta plus decay">beta plus decay</a> (with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half_life" title="Half life">half life</a> of six days) and gamma rays. <sup>56</sup>Co itself decays by the beta plus (<a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a>) path with a half life of 77 days into stable <sup>56</sup>Fe. These two processes are responsible for the electromagnetic radiation from Type Ia supernovae. In combination with the changing transparency of the ejected material, they produce the rapidly declining light curve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mazzali_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-mazzali-119">[117]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Core collapse supernovae are on average visually fainter than Type Ia supernovae, but the total energy released is far higher. In these type of supernovae, the gravitational potential energy is converted into kinetic energy that compresses and collapses the core, initially producing <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino">electron neutrinos</a> from disintegrating nucleons, followed by all <a href="/wiki/Flavour_(particle_physics)" title="Flavour (particle physics)">flavours</a> of thermal neutrinos from the super-heated neutron star core. Around 1% of these neutrinos are thought to deposit sufficient energy into the outer layers of the star to drive the resulting catastrophe, but again the details cannot be reproduced exactly in current models. Kinetic energies and nickel yields are somewhat lower than Type Ia supernovae, hence the lower peak visual luminosity of Type II supernovae, but energy from the de-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionisation" title="Ionisation">ionisation</a> of the many solar masses of remaining hydrogen can contribute to a much slower decline in luminosity and produce the plateau phase seen in the majority of core collapse supernovae.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Energetics of supernovae\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Supernova</th>\n<th>Approximate total energy<br/>10<sup>44</sup> joules (<a href="/wiki/Foe_(unit)" title="Foe (unit)">foe</a>)<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_c"><a href="#endnote_c">c</a></sup></th>\n<th>Ejected Ni<br/>(solar masses)</th>\n<th>Neutrino energy<br/>(foe)</th>\n<th>Kinetic energy<br/>(foe)</th>\n<th>Electromagnetic radiation<br/>(foe)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Type Ia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mazzali_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-mazzali-119">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup></td>\n<td style="text-align:right">1.5</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">0.4 &#8211; 0.8</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">0.1</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">1.3 &#8211; 1.4</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">~0.01\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Core collapse<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup></td>\n<td style="text-align:right">100</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">(0.01) &#8211; 1</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">100</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">1</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">0.001 &#8211; 0.01\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Hypernova</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">100</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">~1</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">1&#8211;100</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">1&#8211;100</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">~0.1\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Pair instability<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kasen_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-kasen-90">[88]</a></sup></td>\n<td style="text-align:right">5&#8211;100</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">0.5 &#8211; 50</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">low?</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">1&#8211;100</td>\n<td style="text-align:right">0.01 &#8211; 0.1\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In some core collapse supernovae, fallback onto a black hole drives <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_jet" title="Relativistic jet">relativistic jets</a> which may produce a brief energetic and directional burst of <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a> and also transfers substantial further energy into the ejected material. This is one scenario for producing high luminosity supernovae and is thought to be the cause of Type Ic hypernovae and long duration <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst" title="Gamma-ray burst">gamma-ray bursts</a>. If the relativistic jets are too brief and fail to penetrate the stellar envelope then a low luminosity gamma-ray burst may be produced and the supernova may be sub-luminous.\n</p><p>When a supernova occurs inside a small dense cloud of circumstellar material, it will produce a shock wave that can efficiently convert a high fraction of the kinetic energy into electromagnetic radiation. Even though the initial energy was entirely normal the resulting supernova will have high luminosity and extended duration since it does not rely on exponential radioactive decay. This type of event may cause Type IIn hypernovae.\n</p><p>Although pair-instability supernovae are core collapse supernovae with spectra and light curves similar to Type II-P, the nature after core collapse is more like that of a giant Type Ia with runaway fusion of carbon, oxygen, and silicon. The total energy released by the highest mass events is comparable to other core collapse supernovae but neutrino production is thought to be very low, hence the kinetic and electromagnetic energy released is very high. The cores of these stars are much larger than any white dwarf and the amount of radioactive nickel and other heavy elements ejected from their cores can be orders of magnitude higher, with consequently high visual luminosity.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Progenitor">Progenitor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Progenitor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Artist%27s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm/854px--Artist%27s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="31.2" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Artist&amp;#039;s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/67/Artist%27s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm/Artist%27s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="252640" data-framerate="25"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/67/Artist%27s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm/Artist%27s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm.360p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="74192" data-framerate="25"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/67/Artist%27s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm/Artist%27s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="503352" data-framerate="25"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:Artist\'s impression time-lapse of distant supernovae.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Artist%27s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm/220px--Artist%27s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/67/Artist%27s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_time-lapse_of_distant_supernovae.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shown in this sped-up artist\'s impression, is a collection of distant galaxies, the occasional supernova can be seen. Each of these exploding stars briefly rivals the brightness of its host galaxy.</div></div></div>\n<p>The supernova classification type is closely tied to the type of star at the time of the collapse. The occurrence of each type of supernova depends dramatically on the metallicity, and hence the age of the host galaxy.\n</p><p>Type Ia supernovae are produced from <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> stars in <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary</a> systems and occur in all <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_morphological_classification" title="Galaxy morphological classification">galaxy types</a>. Core collapse supernovae are only found in galaxies undergoing current or very recent star formation, since they result from short-lived massive stars. They are most commonly found in Type Sc <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxy" title="Spiral galaxy">spirals</a>, but also in the arms of other spiral galaxies and in <a href="/wiki/Irregular_galaxy" title="Irregular galaxy">irregular galaxies</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Starburst_galaxy" title="Starburst galaxy">starburst galaxies</a>.\n</p><p>Type Ib/c and II-L, and possibly most Type IIn, supernovae are only thought to be produced from stars having near-solar metallicity levels that result in high mass loss from massive stars, hence they are less common in older, more-distant galaxies. The table shows the expected progenitor for the main types of core collapse supernova, and the approximate proportions that have been observed in the local neighbourhood.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Fraction of core collapse supernovae types by progenitor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eldridge_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-eldridge-97">[95]</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Type</th>\n<th>Progenitor star</th>\n<th>Fraction\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Ib</td>\n<td>WC <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf&#8211;Rayet star">Wolf&#8211;Rayet</a> or <a href="/wiki/Helium_star" title="Helium star">helium star</a></td>\n<td>9.0%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Ic</td>\n<td>WO <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf&#8211;Rayet star">Wolf&#8211;Rayet</a></td>\n<td>17.0%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>II-P</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supergiant" title="Supergiant">Supergiant</a></td>\n<td>55.5%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>II-L</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supergiant" title="Supergiant">Supergiant</a> with a depleted hydrogen shell</td>\n<td>3.0%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>IIn</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supergiant" title="Supergiant">Supergiant</a> in a dense cloud of expelled material (such as <a href="/wiki/Luminous_blue_variable" title="Luminous blue variable">LBV</a>)</td>\n<td>2.4%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>IIb</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supergiant" title="Supergiant">Supergiant</a> with highly depleted hydrogen (stripped by companion?)</td>\n<td>12.1%\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>IIpec</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_supergiant" title="Blue supergiant">Blue supergiant</a>?</td>\n<td>1.0%\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>There are a number of difficulties reconciling modelled and observed stellar evolution leading up to core collapse supernovae. Red supergiants are the expected progenitors for the vast majority of core collapse supernovae, and these have been observed but only at relatively low masses and luminosities, below about 18&#160;<var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub> and 100,000&#160;<var>L</var><sub>&#9737;</sub> respectively. Most progenitors of Type II supernovae are not detected and must be considerably fainter, and presumably less massive. It is now proposed that higher mass red supergiants do not explode as supernovae, but instead evolve back towards hotter temperatures. Several progenitors of Type IIb supernovae have been confirmed, and these were K and G supergiants, plus one A supergiant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smartt_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-smartt-124">[122]</a></sup> Yellow hypergiants or LBVs are proposed progenitors for Type IIb supernovae, and almost all Type IIb supernovae near enough to observe have shown such progenitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-problem_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-problem-125">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ysg_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-ysg-126">[124]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until just a few decades ago, hot supergiants were not considered likely to explode, but observations have shown otherwise. Blue supergiants form an unexpectedly high proportion of confirmed supernova progenitors, partly due to their high luminosity and easy detection, while not a single Wolf&#8211;Rayet progenitor has yet been clearly identified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smartt_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-smartt-124">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup> Models have had difficulty showing how blue supergiants lose enough mass to reach supernova without progressing to a different evolutionary stage. One study has shown a possible route for low-luminosity post-red supergiant luminous blue variables to collapse, most likely as a Type IIn supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lbv_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-lbv-128">[126]</a></sup> Several examples of hot luminous progenitors of Type IIn supernovae have been detected: <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SN_2005gy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SN 2005gy (page does not exist)">SN 2005gy</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SN_2010jl&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SN 2010jl (page does not exist)">SN 2010jl</a> were both apparently massive luminous stars, but are very distant; and <a href="/wiki/SN_2009ip" title="SN 2009ip">SN 2009ip</a> had a highly luminous progenitor likely to have been an LBV, but is a peculiar supernova whose exact nature is disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smartt_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-smartt-124">[122]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The progenitors of Type Ib/c supernovae are not observed at all, and constraints on their possible luminosity are often lower than those of known WC stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smartt_124-3"><a href="#cite_note-smartt-124">[122]</a></sup> WO stars are extremely rare and visually relatively faint, so it is difficult to say whether such progenitors are missing or just yet to be observed. Very luminous progenitors have not been securely identified, despite numerous supernovae being observed near enough that such progenitors would have been clearly imaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yoon_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-yoon-129">[127]</a></sup> Population modelling shows that the observed Type Ib/c supernovae could be reproduced by a mixture of single massive stars and stripped-envelope stars from interacting binary systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eldridge_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-eldridge-97">[95]</a></sup> The continued lack of unambiguous detection of progenitors for normal Type Ib and Ic supernovae may be due to most massive stars collapsing directly to a black hole <a href="/wiki/Failed_supernova" title="Failed supernova">without a supernova outburst</a>. Most of these supernovae are then produced from lower-mass low-luminosity helium stars in binary systems. A small number would be from rapidly-rotating massive stars, likely corresponding to the highly-energetic Type Ic-BL events that are associated with long-duration <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst" title="Gamma-ray burst">gamma-ray bursts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smartt_124-4"><a href="#cite_note-smartt-124">[122]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstellar_impact">Interstellar impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Interstellar impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Source_of_heavy_elements">Source of heavy elements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Source of heavy elements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">Supernova nucleosynthesis</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1509" data-file-width="1510" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg/220px-An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg/330px-An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg/440px-An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Isolated neutron star in the <a href="/wiki/Small_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Small Magellanic Cloud">Small Magellanic Cloud</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Supernovae are the major source of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> heavier than <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup> These elements are produced by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> for nuclei up to <sup>34</sup>S, by silicon photodisintegration rearrangement and quasiequilibrium (see <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">Supernova nucleosynthesis</a>) during silicon burning for nuclei between <sup>36</sup>Ar and <sup>56</sup>Ni, and by rapid captures of neutrons during the supernova\'s collapse for elements heavier than iron. Nucleosynthesis during silicon burning yields nuclei roughly 1000-100,000 times more abundant than the r-process isotopes heavier than iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup> Supernovae are the most likely, although not undisputed, candidate sites for the <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">r-process</a>, which is the rapid capture of neutrons that occurs at high temperature and high density of neutrons. Those reactions produce highly unstable <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a> that are rich in <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> and that rapidly <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> into more stable forms. The <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">r-process</a> produces about half of all the heavier isotopes of the elements beyond iron, including <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup> The only other major competing process for producing elements heavier than iron is the <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a> in large, old, red-giant AGB stars, which produces these elements slowly over longer epochs and which cannot produce elements heavier than <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_stellar_evolution">Role in stellar evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Role in stellar evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">Supernova remnant</a></div>\n<p>Remnants of many supernovae consist of a compact object and a rapidly expanding <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a> of material. This cloud of material sweeps up the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> during a free expansion phase, which can last for up to two centuries. The wave then gradually undergoes a period of <a href="/wiki/Adiabatic_process" title="Adiabatic process">adiabatic expansion</a>, and will slowly cool and mix with the surrounding interstellar medium over a period of about 10,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STScl-2005-15.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="658" data-file-width="867" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/STScl-2005-15.png/220px-STScl-2005-15.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/STScl-2005-15.png/330px-STScl-2005-15.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/STScl-2005-15.png/440px-STScl-2005-15.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STScl-2005-15.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Supernova remnant N 63A lies within a clumpy region of gas and dust in the <a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large Magellanic Cloud</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> produced <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, and traces of <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, while all heavier elements are synthesized in stars and supernovae. Supernovae tend to enrich the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> with elements other than hydrogen and helium, which usually astronomers refer to as "<a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metals</a>".\n</p><p>These injected elements ultimately enrich the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular clouds</a> that are the sites of star formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup> Thus, each stellar generation has a slightly different composition, going from an almost pure mixture of hydrogen and helium to a more metal-rich composition. Supernovae are the dominant mechanism for distributing these heavier elements, which are formed in a star during its period of nuclear fusion. The different abundances of elements in the material that forms a star have important influences on the star\'s life, and may decisively influence the possibility of having <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> orbiting it.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of an expanding supernova remnant can trigger star formation by compressing nearby, dense molecular clouds in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup> The increase in turbulent pressure can also prevent star formation if the cloud is unable to lose the excess energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa128_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-aaa128-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Evidence from daughter products of short-lived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive_isotope" title="Radioactive isotope">radioactive isotopes</a> shows that a nearby supernova helped determine the composition of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> 4.5 billion years ago, and may even have triggered the formation of this system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> Supernova production of heavy elements over astronomic periods of time ultimately made the <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">chemistry of life</a> on Earth possible.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_Earth">Effect on Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Effect on Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_supernova" title="Near-Earth supernova">Near-Earth supernova</a></div>\n<p>A <b>near-Earth supernova</b> is a supernova close enough to the Earth to have noticeable effects on its <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>. Depending upon the type and energy of the supernova, it could be as far as 3000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> away. <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">Gamma rays</a> from a supernova would induce a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reaction</a> in the upper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth\'s atmosphere">atmosphere</a> converting molecular <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> into <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxides</a>, depleting the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a> enough to expose the surface to harmful <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a>. This has been proposed as the cause of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordovician%E2%80%93Silurian_extinction" title="Ordovician&#8211;Silurian extinction">Ordovician&#8211;Silurian extinction</a>, which resulted in the death of nearly 60% of the oceanic life on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup>\nIn 1996 it was theorized that traces of past supernovae might be detectable on Earth in the form of metal isotope signatures in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_strata" title="Rock strata">rock strata</a>. <a href="/wiki/Iron#Isotopes" title="Iron">Iron-60</a> enrichment was later reported in deep-sea rock of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fields-142">[140]</a></sup> In 2009, elevated levels of nitrate ions were found in Antarctic ice, which coincided with the 1006 and 1054 supernovae. Gamma rays from these supernovae could have boosted levels of nitrogen oxides, which became trapped in the ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Type Ia supernovae are thought to be potentially the most dangerous if they occur close enough to the Earth. Because these supernovae arise from dim, common white dwarf stars in binary systems, it is likely that a supernova that can affect the Earth will occur unpredictably and in a star system that is not well studied. The closest known candidate is <a href="/wiki/IK_Pegasi" title="IK Pegasi">IK Pegasi</a> (see below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup> Recent estimates predict that a Type II supernova would have to be closer than eight <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a> (26 light-years) to destroy half of the Earth\'s ozone layer, and there are no such candidates closer than about 500 light years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gehrels_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrels-145">[143]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Milky_Way_candidates">Milky Way candidates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Milky Way candidates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_supernova_candidates" title="List of supernova candidates">List of supernova candidates</a></div>\n<p>The next supernova in the Milky Way will likely be detectable even if it occurs on the far side of the galaxy. It is likely to be produced by the collapse of an unremarkable red supergiant and it is very probable that it will already have been catalogued in infrared surveys such as <a href="/wiki/2MASS" title="2MASS">2MASS</a>. There is a smaller chance that the next core collapse supernova will be produced by a different type of massive star such as a yellow hypergiant, luminous blue variable, or Wolf&#8211;Rayet. The chances of the next supernova being a Type Ia produced by a white dwarf are calculated to be about a third of those for a core collapse supernova. Again it should be observable wherever it occurs, but it is less likely that the progenitor will ever have been observed. It isn\'t even known exactly what a Type Ia progenitor system looks like, and it is difficult to detect them beyond a few parsecs. The total supernova rate in our galaxy is estimated to be between 2 and 12 per century, although we haven\'t actually observed one for several centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-adams-105">[103]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wolf_rayet2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="641" data-file-width="611" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Wolf_rayet2.jpg/220px-Wolf_rayet2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Wolf_rayet2.jpg/330px-Wolf_rayet2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Wolf_rayet2.jpg/440px-Wolf_rayet2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wolf_rayet2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_nebula" title="Wolf&#8211;Rayet nebula">nebula</a> around <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf&#8211;Rayet star">Wolf&#8211;Rayet star</a> WR124, which is located at a distance of about 21,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_year" title="Light year">light years</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Statistically, the next supernova is likely to be produced from an otherwise unremarkable red supergiant, but it is difficult to identify which of those supergiants are in the final stages of heavy element fusion in their cores and which have millions of years left. The most-massive red supergiants are expected to shed their atmospheres and evolve to Wolf&#8211;Rayet stars before their cores collapse. All Wolf&#8211;Rayet stars are expected to end their lives from the Wolf&#8211;Rayet phase within a million years or so, but again it is difficult to identify those that are closest to core collapse. One class that is expected to have no more than a few thousand years before exploding are the WO Wolf&#8211;Rayet stars, which are known to have exhausted their core helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup> Only eight of them are known, and only four of those are in the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tramper_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-tramper-148">[146]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A number of close or well known stars have been identified as possible core collapse supernova candidates: the red supergiants <a href="/wiki/Antares" title="Antares">Antares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Betelgeuse" title="Betelgeuse">Betelgeuse</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup> the yellow hypergiant <a href="/wiki/Rho_Cassiopeiae" title="Rho Cassiopeiae">Rho Cassiopeiae</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup> the luminous blue variable <a href="/wiki/Eta_Carinae" title="Eta Carinae">Eta Carinae</a> that has already produced a <a href="/wiki/Supernova_impostor" title="Supernova impostor">supernova impostor</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup> and the brightest component, a <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf&#8211;Rayet star">Wolf&#8211;Rayet star</a>, in the Regor or <a href="/wiki/Gamma_Velorum" title="Gamma Velorum">Gamma Velorum</a> system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup> Others have gained notoriety as possible, although not very likely, progenitors for a gamma-ray burst; for example <a href="/wiki/WR_104" title="WR 104">WR 104</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Identification of candidates for a Type Ia supernova is much more speculative. Any binary with an accreting white dwarf might produce a supernova although the exact mechanism and timescale is still debated. These systems are faint and difficult to identify, but the novae and recurrent novae are such systems that conveniently advertise themselves. One example is <a href="/wiki/U_Scorpii" title="U Scorpii">U Scorpii</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup> The nearest known Type Ia supernova candidate is <a href="/wiki/IK_Pegasi" title="IK Pegasi">IK Pegasi</a> (HR 8210), located at a distance of 150 light-years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup> but observations suggest it will be several million years before the white dwarf can accrete the critical mass required to become a Type Ia supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="metadata mbox-small" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Monthly+Notices+of+the+Royal+Astronomical+Society&amp;rft.atitle=On+the+empirical+evidence+for+the+existence+of+ultramassive+white+dwarfs&amp;rft.volume=389&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=1367&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft_id=info%3Aarxiv%2F0806.4742&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1111%2Fj.1365-2966.2008.13652.x&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2008MNRAS.389.1367V&amp;rft.aulast=Vennes&amp;rft.aufirst=S.&amp;rft.au=Kawka%2C+A.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASupernova"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n</div>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The value is obtained by converting the suffix "nc" from <a href="/wiki/Bijective_numeration#The_bijective_base-26_system" title="Bijective numeration">bijective base-26</a>, with <i>a</i>=1, <i>b</i>=2, <i>c</i>=3, ... <i>z</i>=26. Thus nc=<i>n</i> &#215; 26 + <i>c</i>=14 &#215; 26 + 3=367.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a core primarily composed of oxygen, neon and magnesium, the collapsing white dwarf will typically form a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a>. In this case, only a fraction of the star\'s mass will be ejected during the collapse.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://heasarc.gsfc.nasa.gov/docs/objects/snrs/snrstext.html" rel="nofollow">"Introduction to Supernova Remnants"</a>. <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>/<a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">GSFC</a>. 2007-10-04<span class="reference-accessdate">.
